<span id="freeTextContainer17614535274775009252"><b>Title: Faust<br/><br/>Author: Johann Wolfgang von Goethe<br/><br/>Illustrator: Eugène Delacroix<br/><br/>Translator: Albert Stapfer<br/><br/>Release Date: February 19, 2017 [EBook #54202]<br/><br/>Language: French<br/><br/>Produced by Laura N.R. & Marc D'Hooghe at Free<br/>Literature (online soon in an extended version, also linking<br/>to free sources for education worldwide ... MOOC's,<br/>educational materials,...) Images generously made available<br/>by the Gallica, Bibliothèque nationale de France.</b><br/><br/>Free download available at <a href='http://www.gutenberg.org/ebooks/54202' rel='nofollow noopener'>Project Gutenberg</a>.<br/><br/>I made the proofing for Fre</span>
  <span id="freeText17614535274775009252" style="display:none"><b>Title: Faust<br /><br />Author: Johann Wolfgang von Goethe<br /><br />Illustrator: Eugène Delacroix<br /><br />Translator: Albert Stapfer<br /><br />Release Date: February 19, 2017 [EBook #54202]<br /><br />Language: French<br /><br />Produced by Laura N.R. &amp; Marc D'Hooghe at Free<br />Literature (online soon in an extended version, also linking<br />to free sources for education worldwide ... MOOC's,<br />educational materials,...) Images generously made available<br />by the Gallica, Bibliothèque nationale de France.</b><br /><br />Free download available at <a href="http://www.gutenberg.org/ebooks/54202" rel="nofollow noopener">Project Gutenberg</a>.<br /><br />I made the proofing for Free Literature and it will be published by Project Gutenberg. <br /><br />The original file is provided by Gallica - Biblioteque Nationale de France.<br /><br /><b>Preface:<br />Il est certains poèmes qui ont eu de tout temps le privilége, pour ainsi dire exclu- sif, d'éveiller l'imagination des peintres; tels sont, entre autres et par excellence, l'Iliade et l'Odyssée d'Homère, le Paradis perdu de Milton, le Roland furieux du divin Arioste on ferait un volume avec la simple énumération des tableaux ou des- sins remarquables qu'ils ont inspirés depuis leur apparition jusqu'à nos jours. Parmi les compositions poétiques tout-à-fait modernes, qui, sous ce rapport, méritent de lutter avec celles que nous venons de nommer, le Faust de M. de Goethe doit être mis au premier rang.</b><br /><br />Notes:<br />Ce qui a été publié de Faust, n'est effectivement qu'une <i>première partie</i> du vaste drame, dont la vie de ce personnage, à partir de l'instant où il engage son âme, devait faire le sujet; car, à la fin de<br />la dernière scène, loin de l'emporter aux enfers en l'emmenant avec lui, le Diable l'arrache ainsi, au contraire, à la mort inévitable qu'il eût trouvée, s'il fût demeuré plus long-temps dans le cachot de<br />Marguerite. Néanmoins comme, d'une part, en se décidant à continuer de vivre dans la compagnie de Méphistophélès, le docteur Faust consomme sa perdition; et que, de l'autre, après avoir inutilement attendu pendant quarante années la <i>seconde partie</i> de l'ouvrage, le public commençait à<br />en désespérer absolument, nous allions effacer ce titre; quand, tout d'un coup, la publication de cette seconde partie nous fût annoncée par l'auteur lui-même: l'effacer malgré cela, c'eût été reculer devant l'espèce d'engagement qu'un tel titre nous faisait prendre, et que nous aimions à contracter, de donner, un jour, un pendant au présent volume; nous l'avons donc laissé subsister. Voici un extrait de la lettre que M. de Goethe nous fit l'honneur de nous adresser à ce sujet, le 4 avril 1827. Ayant, à cette époque, ouï dire qu'il se proposait de publier incessamment une scène, jusque-là inédite, de Faust, nous l'avions prié d'avoir la bonté de nous la communiquer, afin que nous pussions en joindre la traduction à celle du reste de l'ouvrage: «Dans ce moment,» nous répondit-il, «il ne sera rien ajouté à la <i>première partie</i> de Faust, que vous avez eu' l'obligeance de traduire; «elle restera<br />absolument telle qu'elle est. Le nouveau drame que j'ai annoncé, sous le titre d’<i>Hélène</i>, est un intermède appartenant à la <i>seconde partie</i>; et cette seconde partie est complètement différente de la première, soit pour le plan, soit pour l'exécution, soit enfin pour le lieu de la scène, qui est placé dans <i>des régions plus élevées.</i> Elle n'est point encore terminée; et c'est comme échantillon seulement, que je publie l’<i>intermède d'Hélène</i>, lequel doit y entrer plus tard. La presque totalité de cet intermède est écrite en vers ïambiques, et autres vers employés par les anciens, dont il n'y a pas trace dans la première partie de Faust. Vous vous convaincrez vous-même, quand vous le lirez, qu'il ne peut en aucune façon se rattacher à la première partie, et que M. Motte nuirait au succès de sa publication, s'il voulait essayer de l'y joindre. Mais si, après l'avoir lu, vous le trouvez assez de<br />votre goût, pour avoir envie de le traduire; s'il inspire, en outre, quelque artiste, qui se sente le talent comme le désir d'en crayonner les diverses situations; et si, enfin, de son côté, M. Motte ne répugne<br />pas à publier ce nouvel ouvrage: je vous garantis qu'il pourra se suffire à lui-même. Car, ainsi que je l'ai déjà dit, et que vous le verrez bientôt par vos yeux, il forme un tout complet et a une étendue<br />convenable, etc.»</span>
  <a data-text-id="17614535274775009252" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3101531399" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3101531399" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31667052-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589658539p2/31667052.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3101531399?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/31667052-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780672604140" href="/book/show/1188965.Faust_I_II">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3101531399" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2280021654190374147"> <i>All in earth’s fleeting state <br/> As symbol showeth;<br/> Here, the inadequate<br/> To fullness groweth;<br/> Here is wrought the ineffable,<br/> Through heavenly love;<br/> The Ever-Womanly<br/> Draws us above.</i><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1579083684i/28781324.jpg' width='200” height=' alt='Salvador Dali Faust' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/285217.Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe' title='Johann Wolfgang von Goethe' rel='nofollow noopener'>Johann Wolfgang von Goethe</a> brought together allegory, mythology, and elements of magical realism to create his epic play <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/406373.Faust' title='Faust by Johann Wolfgang von Goethe' rel='nofollow noopener'>Faust</a> <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/406373.Faust' title='Faust by Johann Wolfgang von Goethe' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1320404568l/406373._SY75_.jpg' alt='Faust by Johann Wolfgang von Goethe' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a>.<br/><br/>A very brief synopsis is: Faust, a learned gentleman, is burned out and seeks more in life. He feels like something is missing in his life, so he makes a deal with the devil to sell his so</span>
  <span id="freeText2280021654190374147" style="display:none"> <i>All in earth’s fleeting state <br> As symbol showeth;<br> Here, the inadequate<br> To fullness groweth;<br> Here is wrought the ineffable,<br> Through heavenly love;<br> The Ever-Womanly<br> Draws us above.</i><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1579083684i/28781324.jpg" width="200” height=" alt="Salvador Dali Faust" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/285217.Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe" rel="nofollow noopener">Johann Wolfgang von Goethe</a> brought together allegory, mythology, and elements of magical realism to create his epic play <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/406373.Faust" title="Faust by Johann Wolfgang von Goethe" rel="nofollow noopener">Faust</a> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/406373.Faust" title="Faust by Johann Wolfgang von Goethe" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1320404568l/406373._SY75_.jpg" alt="Faust by Johann Wolfgang von Goethe" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a>.<br><br>A very brief synopsis is: Faust, a learned gentleman, is burned out and seeks more in life. He feels like something is missing in his life, so he makes a deal with the devil to sell his soul if the devil can give him something which he never wants to end. The devil takes the deal and the play follows different ways that Faust seeks that ultimate carnal knowledge and utopia which he thinks his soul is lacking. Will he find what he’s looking for? <br><br>The character Mephistopheles (the devil incarnate) was interesting and entertaining. I have to say that I enjoyed the 1st part of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/406373.Faust" title="Faust by Johann Wolfgang von Goethe" rel="nofollow noopener">Faust</a> much more than the 2nd half. The 1st part made more sense and was easier to follow. The 2nd part was honestly over my head. When I get into Greek mythology and references to that I just have a lot of trouble following and comprehending (or caring for that matter). It was very difficult for me to slog through certain parts. <br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/406373.Faust" title="Faust by Johann Wolfgang von Goethe" rel="nofollow noopener">Faust</a> had its moments of brilliance. The contrasting of the characters Faust and Homunculus was interesting. The play also felt a lot like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8852.Macbeth" title="Macbeth by William Shakespeare" rel="nofollow noopener">Macbeth</a> with the witches and Walpurgis Night scenes. <br><br>Would I recommend <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/406373.Faust" title="Faust by Johann Wolfgang von Goethe" rel="nofollow noopener">Faust</a>? Yes on part 1 (which stands by itself). Part 2 gets awfully heavy so I can’t say I’d recommend it unless to completists who want to finish the whole play just for the sake of finishing. <br><br><b>SIDE NOTE</b> This will complete either B3 (Classic published over 200 years ago) or I3 (Classic Play or Drama) for the 2020 Classics Bingo Challenge I’m doing with the Catching up on Classics group here on Goodreads. <br><br><br><br><br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="2280021654190374147" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1970809866" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1970809866" />
    <a title="Michael Finocchiaro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48483884-michael-finocchiaro"><img alt="Michael Finocchiaro" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1576692048p2/48483884.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1970809866?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Finocchiaro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Finocchiaro" href="/user/show/48483884-michael-finocchiaro">Michael Finocchiaro</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780691162294" href="/book/show/18730705-faust-i-ii">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=german-18th-century">german-18th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1970809866" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11433131035532269812">I love the Faust myth by Goethe. It has engendered hundreds of imitations in literature (my favorite being Thomas Mann's Dr Faustus) and opera (Busoni's is the craziest, Gounod's probably the loudest) and movies (well, too many to even name). I have read various English translations and never been able to read the original German much to my regret. Nonetheless, it is an essential read.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_1970809866'><a id="like_count_review_1970809866" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1970809866?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">38 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F406373.Faust">Like</a></span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2376569662" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2376569662" />
    <a title="sologdin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5610133-sologdin"><img alt="sologdin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307928658p2/5610133.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2376569662?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="sologdin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="sologdin" href="/user/show/5610133-sologdin">sologdin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5610133-sologdin?shelf=of-best-sentence-and-moost-solaas">of-best-sentence-and-moost-solaas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5610133-sologdin?shelf=sympathy-for-the-devil">sympathy-for-the-devil</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2376569662" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3924980858706400119">The Dedication opens with a proclamation of radical absence: “what I possess seems far away from me, / And what is gone becomes reality” (ll. 31-32).<br/><br/>The Prelude puts a finer point on this absence, voicing several positions simultaneously on class struggle in “the surging rabble [das wogende gedrange]” (61) vs. “give reign to many-throated fantasy [lasst phantasie mit allen ihren choren]” (86) and “something for all classes” (98) vs. “do not forget for whom you write” (111). The absent masses, an</span>
  <span id="freeText3924980858706400119" style="display:none">The Dedication opens with a proclamation of radical absence: “what I possess seems far away from me, / And what is gone becomes reality” (ll. 31-32).<br /><br />The Prelude puts a finer point on this absence, voicing several positions simultaneously on class struggle in “the surging rabble [das wogende gedrange]” (61) vs. “give reign to many-throated fantasy [lasst phantasie mit allen ihren choren]” (86) and “something for all classes” (98) vs. “do not forget for whom you write” (111). The absent masses, and the absent master.<br /><br />The director chides the writer for writing for an audience containing such persons as “one hopes to play at cards, / Another for an orgy in a harlot’s bed [eine wilde nacht an einer dirne busen]” (125-26), to which the writer responds “seek yourself another slave [andern knecht]” (134), “The noblest right the poet ought to waive? / The right of man that nature granted him, / And waste it frivolously for your gain?” (135-37)—a labor uprising in the play text ab initio. The clown by contrast: “The business of poetry / Conduct as if it were a love affair” (159-60). The director’s final instruction is to “command the muses [commandiert die poesie]” (221). This writing thus knows but one unrest, as two souls war within the text.<br /><br />As in Marlowe, Faust has “studied philosophy, / Jurisprudence and medicine, too, / And, worst of all, theology” (355-57). All of this knowledge is vanity, “nothing” (366); he believes himself “smarter” than his contemporaries (366), and is “not afraid of the Devil or hell” (370). He lacks wealth (375), and wants to “see what secret force / Hides in the world and rules its course” (384-85).<br /><br />Just before the appearance of the proverbial ‘black dog,’ the unequivocal signifier of the demonic (1147):<blockquote>You are aware of only one unrest [cf. RSB];<br />Oh never learn to know the other!<br />Two souls, alas, are dwelling in my breast,<br />And one is striving [NB] to forsake its brother.<br />Unto the world in grossly loving zest,<br />With clinging tendrils, one adheres;<br />The other rises forcibly in quest<br />Of rarefied ancestral spheres. (1110-17)</blockquote>Recall “now I feel unrest awaken [neue trieb enwacht]” (1085); it is apparently “innate in our race [jedem eingeboren] / That our feelings surge” (1092-93).<br /><br />Addressing the populist concern of the preface: “here is the people’s paradise [des volkes wahrer himmel]” (938), referring to resurrection from “lowly houses, musty as stables, / from trades to which they are subjected” (920 ff). Peasant asks Faust not to spurn “dieses Volksgedrang,” the crowd’s desire (983), in wanting to drink with him because he had in the past “always helped in every way” (996), even during the plague (1000 ff).<br /><br />He plainly associates the oikos, "our narrow den" (1194) with how the "light pervades our breast [Busen] again" (1196), signifying a sophrosyne of sorts ("fills the heart that knows itself" (1197)), the condition of possibility for "Reason again begins to speak" (1198)--the repetitive 'again' indicating that outside the oikos, the polis, works against these interests, which he endeavors to seek (1199-1201). Nevertheless, “we are accustomed to see men disdain / What they do not grasp; / When it gives trouble, they profane / Even the beautiful and the good” (1205-08). Even though he has tried, “Contentment flows no longer through my breast [Busen]” (1211). Two souls.<br /><br />Perplexed that a “sacred original text” is now “translated into my beloved German” (1220 ff), he quibbles with John 1:1—“it says ‘In the beginning was the Word.’ / Already I am stopped. It seems absurd” (1224 et seq.). He thereafter renders goal-oriented translations: “the Mind [der Sinn],” “Force [der Kraft],” and finally “the Act [die Tat]”—plainly theory v. praxis, the distinction between Protestant and Catholic soteriology (we recall the antinomian heresy in Hogg).<br /><br />Spirits arrive; he attempts to discipline them: “Do not require [erwarte nicht] / My art in its full measure [die starkste von meinen kunsten]”(1320-21)—whereupon Mephistopheles makes his first appearance in propria persona. M says “You are not yet the man to hold the Devil fast” (1509); “dazzle him with dream shapes [umgaukelt ihn mit sussen traumgestalten], sweet and vast, / Plunge him into an ocean of untruth [Meer des Wahns]” (1510-11)--perhaps worthy of recollection as the play progresses.<br /><br />Faust will “not cease to feel,” “too old to be content,” “too young to be without desire” (1544-47). On the basis of unrequited desire: <blockquote>The god that dwells within my heart [Busen]<br />Can stir my depths, I cannot hide-- <br />Rules all my powers with relentless art, <br />But cannot move the world outside; <br />And thus existence is for me a weight, <br />Death is desirable, and life I hate. (1566-71)</blockquote> Mephisto encourages him to “stop playing with you melancholy / That, like a vulture, ravages your breast” (1635-36), suggesting that he avoid joining “the herd you hate [Pack zu stossen]” (1640)—the conjunction of the populist and sentimental thematics—there is a class basis for emotionalism, apparently, in the doctrine of hell. In this connection, he offers to be Faust’s slave [Knecht] (1648)—on the condition that “when we meet again beyond,‘ Then you shall do the same for me” (1658-59); Faust however: “of the beyond I have no thought” (1660)--disregards the planning typical of instrumental reason for present aesthetics?<br /><br />This Faust distinguishes himself from Marlowe’s insofar as he does not want to recline, “calmed, on a bed of sloth” (1692-93) (Dr. Faustus by contrast wants a bed of roses). Some haggling over the agreement here, which amounts to putting Faust in fetters and freeing Mephisto (1700 ff). M: “Consider it, for we shall not forget it” (1707), but then insists on a writing: “Could you give me a line or two?” (1715). F: “Is not my spoken word sufficient warrant / When it commits my life eternally?” (1718-19)—standard bourgeois in “a parchment, signed and sealed, is an abhorrent / Specter that haunts us” (1726-27). (Another contrast with Marlowe). F: “The word dies when we seize the pen” (1728; cf. 1224!); M: “Why get so hot and overdo / Your rhetoric?” 1734-35)—just “with a drop of blood you sign your name” (1737). F now “I loathe the knowledge I once sought” (1749)—recordation as corruption (Derrida on writing?).<br /><br />F: “no thought of joy” (1765); “cured from the craving to know all” (1769); “I shall enjoy deep in my self, contain / Within my spirit summit and abyss” (1770 ff). F: “I gathered up and piled up high / In vain the treasures of the human mind” (1810-11). This is not an aesthetic concern, but M tries to twist it back into aesthetics: “We have to look at them more shrewdly, / Or all life’s pleasures pass us by” (1818-19). M otherwise contends <blockquote>Have contempt for reason and for science, <br />Man’s noblest force spurn with defiance, <br />Subscribe o magic and illusion, <br />The Lord of Lies aids your confusion,<br />And, pact or no, I hold you tight. (1851-55)</blockquote> Regarding the study of law, M regards it as a “blight” (1971); theology has “lots of hidden poison” that “one can scarcely tell from medicine” (1985 ff), a true derridean pharmakon. On certainty itself, M opines “the proper word is never far. / With words a dispute can be won, / With words a system can be spun” (1996-98). On medicine as a study, M: after suggesting seduction of female patients, “gray, my dear friend, is every theory” (2038).<br /><br />Though he detests “crazy sorcery” (2337), Faust wants to reduce his age by 30 years (2342); M advises that no sorcery is necessary—rather, “Live simply and keep all your thoughts / On a few simple objects glued; / Restrict yourself and eat the plainest food; / Live with the beasts, a beast” (2355 et seq.). F is unable to do this, or unwilling—sorcery as very plainly cipher for labor power.<br /><br />“One last look at the mirror where I stood! / So beauteous was that woman’s form!” (2599-2600). M: “You’ll soon find, with this potion’s aid, / Helen of Troy in every maid” (2603-04)—a direct contrast with Marlowe’s famous scene about Helen, wherein there can be no fungibility. This fungibility potion bears fruits in the next scene, which features Gretchen – “virtuous and pure” (2611) as well as “her curtness and her brevity / Was sheer enchanting ecstasy” (2617-18). <br /><br />In seeing her, F: “Poor Faust! I do not know you any more. / Do magic smells surround me here? / Immediate pleasure was my bent, / But now—in dreams of love I’m all but spent” (2720-23).<br /><br />Gretchen is less interested in the abstractions, and more in the class basis: “what good is beauty, even youth?” (2798). “On gold depend / all things. Woe to us poor” (2803-04). Faust however will throw over all his intellectual ambition for “one glance from you, one word gives far more pleasure / Than all the wisdom in the world” (3079-80) (cf. 1224). F to G: “Let this sweet flower’s word / Be as God’s word to you” (3184-85). (cf. 1224: much instability here).<br /><br />Faust has been given “all / That I have asked,” including “Strength to experience her, enjoy her” (3217 ff.), and asks to “penetrate” her “heart [NB]." But some instability here when Faust complains “Damnable fiend! Get yourself hence, / And do not name the beautiful maid! / Let not the lust for her sweet limbs invade / And ravish once again my frenzied sense!” (3326-29). Two souls war within his breast indeed! As in Dante, Faust may be subjugating reason to appetite?<br /><br />“I am near her, however far I be, / She’ll never be forgotten or ignored” (3332-33). “Am I not fugitive?” (3348) (cf. Marlowe)—radical absence, as in the dedication. “I’m restless through and through” (4006) (cf. 1110—unrest even now).<br /><br />Too much. Like a mountain overwhelms. Infinite riches in a little room. Wealth hid within the massy entrails of the earth. Too much.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3924980858706400119" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_209140976" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/209140976" />
    <a title="Caroline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5261455-caroline"><img alt="Caroline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506384653p2/5261455.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/209140976?book_show_action=true">Sep 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Caroline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Caroline" href="/user/show/5261455-caroline">Caroline</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393092080" href="/book/show/474892.Faust">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5261455-caroline?shelf=philip-ward-500-books">philip-ward-500-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5261455-caroline?shelf=german-east-europe">german-east-europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5261455-caroline?shelf=drama">drama</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer209140976" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4426164382719786962">This is a review of the Walter Arndt translation in the Norton Critical Edition.<br/><br/>This was a challenge, both in making myself tackle Part 2 as well as Part 1, and in choosing the most difficult of the translations that I have collected over the years. But this translation tries to hew as closely as possible to Goethe’s range of meters and rhyme, and I think diction, which I hope conveys Goethe’s art most closely. At the same time, the resulting English does not read easily, which means that I prob</span>
  <span id="freeText4426164382719786962" style="display:none">This is a review of the Walter Arndt translation in the Norton Critical Edition.<br /><br />This was a challenge, both in making myself tackle Part 2 as well as Part 1, and in choosing the most difficult of the translations that I have collected over the years. But this translation tries to hew as closely as possible to Goethe’s range of meters and rhyme, and I think diction, which I hope conveys Goethe’s art most closely. At the same time, the resulting English does not read easily, which means that I probably read most pages twice. But well worth it. <br /><br />A very great bonus here is the Norton critical edition. Arndt’s article <i>Translating Faust</i>, which follows Part 2, is fantastic. He is so articulate and reasoned in his opinion that you must translate as closely as possible to the form of the original. <br /><br /><blockquote>The linguistically innocent critic of translations in verse seldom realizes that formal elements, especially rhyme, “tyrannize,” (i.e., co-determine) the original poems as much as the translated ones. They do, of course, and properly so; for it implies no reproach to either poet or translator to recognize that assonance, sonority, rhythm, rhyme, on the one hand, and syntax, grammar, phonology, semasiology of the linguistic code, on the other, are all hierarchical degrees and ranges of restriction. They are weights, arms, and torques of the artistic balance between freedom and necessity of expression.</blockquote><br /><br />Luckily he is also a very good poet, so that while the language may at times be a little difficult to penetrate, it is almost never lead-footed or clumsy. Much of the poetry is truly beautiful. Arndt is also funny and scathing in his attitude toward those who translate more loosely, never hesitating to name names. He must have been a real popular guy at academic conferences. Even if you are not ready to read Faust right now, I highly commend Arndt’s essay as an approach to considering translation in general, and as an example of what the best critical writing can be.<br /><br /><blockquote>{after trashing Lowell’s ‘versions’ and prose versions of long poetic works] It is perfectly obvious why Richard Wilbur wanted to, and had to, cast his new English renditions of Moliere meticulously in metric forms like the original--a feat of art and craft several magnitudes above that of a prose rendering in difficulty, but infinitely worth it, infinitely finer in effect. To an artist who has it at his command, and to the reader or listener who knows the original, it was clearly the sole solution which could do elementary justice to the stringent demands of the model. As for negative examples--which could be more extreme (perhaps second to, perhaps next to, Nabokov’s <i>Onegin</i>) than Fairley’s Faust? What could be a more saddening act, on the part of a revered Goethe scholar, than this methodical wrecking operation, performed by the ponderous steel ball of a paradoxical “prose accuracy,” upon the whole splendidly intricate body of Goethe’s metric architecture? What could be plainer than the fact that in the transference, the bringing home of a work of poetry from another language, fidelity and prose are mutually exclusive goals? Only the sort of musty, once-modish prejudice aired in TLS offers some clue to why many translators and reviewers seem unable to grasp that simple truth...It is more important in translating Faust than, possibly, other narrative and dramatic poetry to follow the poet’s changes of meter and level of diction very strictly. Besides all else it is, this poem is a sovereign <i>Glasperlenspiel</i>, by the <i>magister ludi</i> of German literature...</blockquote><br /><br />Arndt, of course, has the luxury of writing for an audience that is highly motivated to work through his poetry. Most of his readers will already know the poem, or will be in a classroom with an instructor guiding them scene by scene. I have to say that in other situations, with other writers, a version that comes closer to the audience’s ability and more limited willingness to do some work would probably justify a different approach. I had to have help in understanding what was going on, either from other translations or from another included essay in this edition that summarizes the poem, Hermann Weigand’s <i>’Goethe’s Faust: An Introduction’</i>. There are also footnotes on the relevant pages, and 35 pages of Interpretive Notes in a font so small I cannot bring myself to read them. That is one drawback of the edition; the font is small and the pages dense. Both contributed to my inability to read for more than 15 pages or so at a time.<br /><br />My main intent in this review is to offer a comparison of the several different translations I own, to help potential readers sort out which one they might want. But I also want to mention the wealth of critical material in the second half of the edition. It includes the essay by Arndt, an accompanying very nice essay by the editor, <i>Reading Faust</i>, the text of the original Faust puppet play, related readings from Goethe on the Faust (including letters and conversations with Eckermann), several excerpts from Goethe’s contemporaries (Schiller, Heine, Schlegel, de Stael, etc.), and modern pieces from Weigand, Hans Mayer, Georg Lukacs and about eight others.<br /><br />So, to the comparison. From Part I, in the study where Faust is about to make his pact with Mephistopheles. First the German, then the translations, moving from the ones that adhere most closely to the original to those that are looser. (I apologize for the lack of diacriticals for the German; I’m too tired to look up the key combinations right now.)<br /><br />Faust:So fluch ich allem, was die Seele<br />Mit Lock- und Gaukelwerk umspannt<br />Und sie in diese Trauervolle<br />Mit Blend- und Schmeichelhaften bannt.<br />Verlucht voraus die hohe Meinung,<br />Womit der Geist sich selbst umfangt!<br />Verflucht das Blenden der Erscheinung,<br />Die sich an unsre Sinne drangt!<br /><br />Arndt (Norton Critical Edition):<br />My curse I hurl on all that spangles<br />The mind with dazzling make-belief,<br />With lures and blandishments entangles<br />The soul within this cave of grief!<br />Accursed, to start, the smug delusion<br />Whereby the mind itself ensnares! <br />Cursed, brash phenomenal intrusion<br />That blinds the senses unawares!<br /><br />Walter Kaufman (Anchor):<br />I now curse all that would enamor<br />The human soul with lures and lies,<br />Enticing it with flattering glamour<br />To live on in this cave of sighs.<br />Cursed above all our high esteem,<br />The spirit’s smug self-confidence,<br />Cursed be illusion, fraud and dream<br />That flatter our guileless sense!<br /><br />Charles E Passage (Bobbs Merril LIbrary of Liberal Arts):<br />I now curse everything that holds the soul<br />Enchanted by the lures of sorcery<br />And charms it in this dreary hole<br />By sweet illusion and duplicity!<br />Cursed be the lofty self-opinion<br />With which the mind itself deludes!<br />Cursed be phenomenon’s dominion<br />Which on our senses so intrudes!<br /><br />David Luke (Oxford):<br />But now I curse all flattering spells<br />That tempt our souls with consolation,<br />All that beguilingly compels <br />Us to endure earth’s tribulations!<br />A curse first on the high presences<br />of our own intellectual pride!<br />A curse on our deluded senses<br />That keep life’s surface beautified!<br /><br />Carlyle F MacIntyre’s ‘New American translation’ for New Directions:<br />now I curse all that holds the soul with lures<br />and hocus-pocus, all that confines it with blinding flattery<br />to this cave of gloom. I curse above all<br />that false self-exaltation with which the mind<br />befuddles itself. Cursed be the blinding<br />of illusion that wraps our senses.<br /><br />I also have a translation of just Part II, by Philip Wayne (Penguin), so I will compare him and Arndt. This is from a scene where Mephistopheles revisits a brash young student he parleyed with in Part I, in Faust’s study.<br /><br />Baccalaureus: Dies ist der Jugend edelster Beruf!<br />Die Welt, sie war nicht, eh’ ich sie erschuf.<br />Die Sonne fuhrt’ ich aus dem Meer herauf.<br />Mit mir begann der Mond des Wechsels Lauf.<br />Da schmuckte sich der Tag auf meinen Wegen,<br />die Erde grünte, blühte mir entengen.<br /><br />Arndt<br />Baccalaureus: Ah, this is youth’s most noble destiny!<br />The world was not until I made it be;<br />I guided up the sun from out the sea;<br />The moon began her changing course with me;<br />And lo! the day adorned itself to meet me,<br />The earth turned green and blossomed forth to greet me.<br /><br />Wayne:<br />This is the noblest call for youthful soul!<br />The world was not, until I made it whole;<br />I raised the sun from oceans where it lay;<br />For me the moon began her changeful way;<br />The day stood forth in beauty at my feet,<br />The green earth blossomed my approach to greet.<br /><br />I also want to mention another resource, particularly if you are going to attempt to read the original German. This is a three volume study version for English-speaking students of German, with extensive introductions and notes in English, the German text, and a full vocabulary volume. It’s <i>Goethe’s Faust</i> by Heffner, Rehder and Twaddell.<br /><br />But wait, you say; you haven’t said a word about the actual thing itself. That is true. After all this reading I am still befuddled. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4426164382719786962" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2195557812" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2195557812" />
    <a title="Manuel Antão" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28709846-manuel-ant-o"><img alt="Manuel Antão" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1504271107p2/28709846.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2195557812?book_show_action=true">Oct 16, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manuel Antão" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manuel Antão" href="/user/show/28709846-manuel-ant-o">Manuel Antão</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780307754974" href="/book/show/11585222-faust">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2195557812" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15028723210292894535">If you're into stuff like this, <a href='http://manuelaantao.blogspot.com/search?q=faust' rel='nofollow noopener'>you can read the full review.</a><br/><br/>To Plug the Mighty Hole Withal: "Faust - First Part" by Goethe<br/><br/><br/><br/>(original review, 2004)<br/><br/><br/>I’m planning on spending a few weeks on Goethe’s Faust in multiple translations and as much of the German as I can manage, supplemented by hundreds of pages of notes and commentary.<br/><br/>I first read the book while in high school in the totally un-annotated Bayard Taylor translation from Modern Library – one of the texts I’m currently reading. <div id="review_194152555" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/194152555" />
    <a title="Paul D.  Miller" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5955255-paul-d-miller"><img alt="Paul D.  Miller" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1453950771p2/5955255.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/194152555?book_show_action=true">Aug 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul D.  Miller" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul D.  Miller" href="/user/show/5955255-paul-d-miller">Paul D.  Miller</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/14704.Faust">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5955255-paul-d-miller?shelf=great-books">great-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer194152555" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13666918343377243899">I hated, hated, hated this book. I liked the language and poetry of the translation I read. I hated the moral. Unreconstructed, unrepentant, gross, nihilistic Romanticism. This book is evil. It teaches that striving is what matters. “Whoever strives in ceaseless toil, him we may grant redemption,” says an angel at the conclusion, justifying Faust's salvation. Faust strives in seduction, fraud, war, debauchery, empire-building, and exploitation of nature. For this he is redeemed. <br/><br/>Apparently the b</span>
  <span id="freeText13666918343377243899" style="display:none">I hated, hated, hated this book. I liked the language and poetry of the translation I read. I hated the moral. Unreconstructed, unrepentant, gross, nihilistic Romanticism. This book is evil. It teaches that striving is what matters. “Whoever strives in ceaseless toil, him we may grant redemption,” says an angel at the conclusion, justifying Faust's salvation. Faust strives in seduction, fraud, war, debauchery, empire-building, and exploitation of nature. For this he is redeemed. <br /><br />Apparently the book is extremely well structured and cultured. The poetry mimics different kinds of classical plays and poems. It is absolutely bursting with allusions and references to classical figures and stories like the guts out of a fat, distended corpse. Who cares? A parade of pedantic trivial minutiae does not gain profundity from its obscurantism. Whole chunks and sections of the book were simply impenetrable without notes. That is less sophisticated writing than the literary equivalent to name-dropping insecurity.<br /><br />One portion truly bothered me. Faust and Helen, representatives of German and Classical culture, marry and have a son. The hybrid is what Faust and a generation of German intellectuals tried to create. Euphorion is a figure of overweening striving, one who reaches beyond the bounds of mortality. The German-classical hybrid, Faust seems to say, is nearly divine. Euphorion plunges to his death—but it is portrayed as heroic. To apotheosize one’s culture—to displace the true divine with one’s own cultural and literary achievements—is to herald fascism. If our culture is the divine one, then the other human cultures are sub-standard, and we have an obligation to spread ours to others, whether others accept it willingly or not. Faust was a proto-fascist.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13666918343377243899" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_145984182" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/145984182" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/145984182?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780691036564" href="/book/show/14703.Faust_I_II">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=rth-lifetime">rth-lifetime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2011">2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=reading-through-history">reading-through-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=faust">faust</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer145984182" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14675260681458327025">This weird, beautiful, complicated play was the work of Goethe's entire life; he wrote it over 60 years, and I doubt he was done when he died. Part II was published posthumously in 1832; it had his, uh, prehumous approval, but that doesn't mean he wouldn't have been happy to spend another ten years tweaking it. To call it an exploration of the Faust myth seems almost like an insult; it's more the distillation of everything he knew and believed, framed loosely by Faust. (And I do mean loosely.)<br/><br/>Th</span>
  <span id="freeText14675260681458327025" style="display:none">This weird, beautiful, complicated play was the work of Goethe's entire life; he wrote it over 60 years, and I doubt he was done when he died. Part II was published posthumously in 1832; it had his, uh, prehumous approval, but that doesn't mean he wouldn't have been happy to spend another ten years tweaking it. To call it an exploration of the Faust myth seems almost like an insult; it's more the distillation of everything he knew and believed, framed loosely by Faust. (And I do mean loosely.)<br /><br />That's undoubtedly part of why it's so complicated. It abruptly switches scenes, themes, tone and meter; sometimes I was halfway through a scene before I even figured out what was going on. It's one of the few works where, at the halfway mark, I was already imagining what it would be like when I read it again. <br /><br />That's a way of saying I didn't get it, and I didn't get it, at least not fully. Hell, it's the entire life of one of our greatest thinkers; I'm not ashamed to admit it. It's also a way of saying that I'm not sure I picked the right translation. I have nothing to compare it to, but Atkins' felt matter-of-fact - plodding - unpoetic. As well, the endnotes and introduction were cursory.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14675260681458327025" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ysE2JIgixwX2+COyJuLapqjcYwWTmjoV6gxdkvCvu6PfK8dQrWk4wpYKEL8kTG9gJFqLREveKawweEy1Mn8GkA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Jp/KLjTZ+EXSCsp3NLFklabkeJTzRDcejMDXDGtMaaczdTtaEZIHgrL4+Xo2H9FTKmKQ1SsAJKdWtMYrqZzUlA==')})
  }
//]]>

Der Text, der sich mit seiner Bezeichnung als &quot;dramatisches Gedicht&quot; der Festlegung auf eine der dramatischen Gattungen entzieht, trug wesentlich dazu bei, den Blankvers als den klassischen deutschen Dramenvers zu etablieren. Mit seinem Nathan reagierte Lessing auf die religiöse Orthodoxie und Intoleranz seiner Zeit. Ort der Handlung ist Jerusalem während der Kreuzzüge – eine Stadt, in der Christentum, Islam und Judentum direkt aufeinandertreffen. Höhepunkt des Stücks, in dem es um eine moral- und geschichtsphilosophische Botschaft, um die Aufforderung zu Toleranz und Humanität geht, ist die berühmte Ringparabel, die der reiche jüdische Kaufmann Nathan erzählt: Sie soll die hintergründige Frage des Sultans Saladin beantworten, welche der drei Religionen die wahre sei.           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9611044240650735507">Johann Wolfgang von Goethe was a German writer. George Eliot called him "Germany's greatest man of letters... and the last true polymath to walk the earth." Goethe's works span the fields of poetry, drama, literature, theology, humanism, and science. Goethe's magnum opus, lauded as one of the peaks of world literature, is the two-part drama <i>Faust</i>. Goethe's other well-known literary works include h</span>
  <span id="freeText9611044240650735507" style="display:none">Johann Wolfgang von Goethe was a German writer. George Eliot called him "Germany's greatest man of letters... and the last true polymath to walk the earth." Goethe's works span the fields of poetry, drama, literature, theology, humanism, and science. Goethe's magnum opus, lauded as one of the peaks of world literature, is the two-part drama <i>Faust</i>. Goethe's other well-known literary works include his numerous poems, the <i>Bildungsroman Wilhelm Meister's Apprenticeship</i> and the epistolary novel <i>The Sorrows of Young Werther</i>.<br /><br />Goethe was one of the key figures of German literature and the movement of Weimar Classicism in the late 18th and early 19th centuries; this movement coincides with Enlightenment, Sentimentality (Empfindsamkeit), Sturm und Drang, and Romanticism. The author of the scientific text Theory of Colours, he influenced Darwin with his focus on plant morphology. He also long served as the Privy Councilor ("Geheimrat") of the duchy of Weimar.<br /><br />Goethe is the originator of the concept of Weltliteratur ("world literature"), having taken great interest in the literatures of England, France, Italy, classical Greece, Persia, Arabic literature, amongst others. His influence on German philosophy is virtually immeasurable, having major impact especially on the generation of Hegel and Schelling, although Goethe himself expressly and decidedly refrained from practicing philosophy in the rarefied sense.<br /><br />Goethe's influence spread across Europe, and for the next century his works were a major source of inspiration in music, drama, poetry and philosophy. Goethe is considered by many to be the most important writer in the German language and one of the most important thinkers in Western culture as well. Early in his career, however, he wondered whether painting might not be his true vocation; late in his life, he expressed the expectation that he would ultimately be remembered above all for his work in optics.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9611044240650735507" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
